So, D_c ≈ (1 + 2) * 0.674 ≈ 3 * 0.674 ≈ 2.022 Gpc.

We can approximate the integral with the trapezoidal rule:

Integral ≈ (x_max - x_min)/2 * [f(x_min) + 2f(x_mid) + f(x_max)]

But since we have three points, maybe use Simpson's rule.

Simpson's rule: Integral ≈ (Delta x)/3 * [f(x0) + 4f(x1) + f(x2)]

Where Delta x = 1, x0=0, x1=0.5, x2=1.

So, Integral ≈ (1)/3 * [1 + 4*(0.577) + 0.516] ≈ (1/3)*(1 + 2.308 + 0.516) ≈ (1/3)*(3.824) ≈ 1.275.

Wait, the integral for comoving distance is ∫0^z 1 / E(z') dz', and E(z)=sqrt(Ω_m(1+z')^3 + Ω_lambda).

In a universe dominated by dark energy, as z increases, E(z) approaches sqrt(Ω_lambda), so the integrand approaches 1 / sqrt(Ω_lambda). So, the integral at high z behaves like ∫1 / sqrt(Ω_lambda) dz, which diverges logarithmically.

Thus, the comoving distance can become large even for high redshifts.

Now, let's approximate the integral using the trapezoidal rule. The trapezoidal rule is:

∫(a to b) f(z) dz ≈ (Δz / 2) [f(z0) + 2f(z1) + 2f(z2) + ... + 2f(z_{n-1}) + f(z_n)]

Where Δz = (b - a)/n, and n is the number of intervals.

Here, n=10 intervals (from z=0 to z=2.1 in steps of 0.2). So, Δz=0.2.

So, the integral ≈ (0.2 / 2) [f(0) + 2f(0.2) + 2f(0.4) + ... + 2f(2.0) + f(2.1)]

So, compute each term:

f(0)=1.0

f(0.2)=1.103

f(0.4)=1.234

f(0.6)=1.389

f(0.8)=1.565

f(1.0)=1.761

For large z, the term x^3 dominates over the constant 7/3, so [x^3 + 7/3] ≈ x^3(1 + (7)/(3x^3)).

Thus, the integral becomes approximately ∫(1 to 1+z) x^{-3/2} [1 - (7)/(6x^3)] dx = ∫(1 to 1+z) x^{-3/2} dx - (7/6) ∫(1 to 1+z) x^{-9/2} dx

Second integral: ∫x^{-9/2} dx = (-2/8) x^{-7/2} + C = (-1/4) x^{-7/2} + C

Thus, the approximation becomes:

≈ [ -2 x^{-1/2} ] from 1 to 1+z - (7/6)[ (-1/4) x^{-7/2} ] from 1 to 1+z

Simplify:

≈ [ -2(1+z)^{-1/2} + 2(1)^{-1/2} ] + (7/24)[ (1+z)^{-7/2} - (1)^{-7/2} ]

= [ -2/(√(1+z)) + 2 ] + (7/24)[ 1/(1+z)^{7/2} - 1 ]

So, combining terms:

≈ 2 - 2/√(1+z) + 7/(24 (1+z)^{7/2}) - 7/24

≈ (2 - 7/24) - 2/√(1+z) + 7/(24 (1+z)^{7/2})

≈ (41/12) - 2/√(1+z) + 7/(24 (1+z)^{7/2})

Okay, so I have this question about a quasar observed in the near infrared and optical spectrum. The peak wavelength is around 790 nm, and the flux drops significantly at shorter wavelengths. I need to figure out why the comoving distance of this quasar from Earth is 9 gigaparsecs (Gpc) in a universe with specific parameters: Hubble constant (H0) is 70 km/s/Mpc, matter density (Omega_m) is 0.3, dark energy density (Omega_lambda) is 0.7, and the universe is flat.

First, let me recall what comoving distance means. Comoving distance is the distance a light signal would travel in a comoving frame, which is a hypothetical frame that expands along with the universe. So, it's essentially the distance to an object as measured in a universe where the expansion has been "stretched out" to make the universe static. This is important because it allows us to use standard distance measurements in a static universe, which simplifies calculations.

The comoving distance (DC) can be calculated using the formula:

DC = (c / H0) * integral from 0 to z of 1 / sqrt(Omega_m(1 + z) + Omega_lambda(1 + z)^2) dz

Where:
- c is the speed of light
- H0 is the Hubble constant
- z is the redshift
- Omega_m and Omega_lambda are the density parameters

But in this case, we don't have the redshift given. Instead, we have the peak wavelength in the observed spectrum. So, maybe I should relate the observed wavelength to the redshift.

The peak wavelength is given as approximately 790 nm. Since quasars are very distant, their light is redshifted. If we knew the rest wavelength of the peak emission, we could calculate the redshift. But wait, the rest wavelength isn't provided. Hmm.

Wait, quasars typically have emission lines, like Lyman-alpha or Balmer series lines. If the peak is at 790 nm, which is in the near-infrared, maybe this corresponds to a specific emission line, say the Hydrogen Lyman-alpha line, which in the rest frame is about 121 nm in the far-ultraviolet. Since 121 nm is shorter than 790 nm, this suggests a significant redshift.

Let me see: if the rest wavelength is 121 nm, then the observed wavelength is 790 nm. The redshift (z) can be calculated using the relation:

lambda_observed = lambda_rest * (1 + z)

So, 790 nm = 121 nm * (1 + z)

Dividing both sides by 121:

790 / 121 = 1 + z

Calculating 790 ÷ 121: Let's see, 121 * 6 = 726, so 121 * 6.5 = 726 + 60.5 = 786.5. Hmm, 121*6.5=786.5, which is close to 790. So, 790 ÷ 121 ≈ 6.528.

So, 1 + z ≈ 6.528 → z ≈ 5.528.

So, z ≈ 5.5. That's a high redshift, meaning the quasar is very far away.

First, from z=0 to z=1:

So, ∫0^1 1 / sqrt(Omega_m*(1 + z')) dz' = ∫0^1 dz' / sqrt(Omega_m*(1 + z')) )

Let me make substitution u = sqrt(1 + z'), then du = 1/(2 sqrt(1 + z')) dz', so dz' = 2 sqrt(1 + z') du

But maybe another substitution: let t = sqrt(1 + z'), so t = sqrt(1 + z'), dt = 1/(2 sqrt(1 + z')) dz', so dz' = 2 dt / t

So, the integral becomes ∫ t=1 to t=sqrt(2) 1/t * 2 dt = 2 ∫1^sqrt(2) dt/t = 2 ln(t) from 1 to sqrt(2) = 2 ln(sqrt(2)) = 2*(1/2) ln(2) = ln(2) ≈ 0.693

So, the integral from z=0 to z=1 is approximately 0.693.

Then, the integral from z=1 to z=5.5: here, dark energy starts to dominate.

In this range, E(z') = sqrt(Omega_m*(1 + z') + Omega_lambda*(1 + z')² )

This integral can be approximated numerically. Let's try to do a rough estimation.

First, note that 0.7x² + 0.3x = x*(0.7x + 0.3). The function inside the sqrt is dominated by the 0.7x² term, so approximately, we can write sqrt(0.7x² + 0.3x) ≈ x * sqrt(0.7 + 0.3/x)

But for x from 2 to 6.5, 0.3/x is from 0.15 to 0.047. So, 0.7 + 0.3/x is between approx 0.7 + 0.047 = 0.747 and 0.7 + 0.15 = 0.85.

So, sqrt(0.7 + 0.3/x) is between sqrt(0.747) ≈ 0.865 and sqrt(0.85) ≈ 0.922.

So, 1 / sqrt(0.7x² + 0.3x) ≈ 1/(x * 0.865 to 0.922) ≈ (1/0.865)/x to (1/0.922)/x ≈ 1.153/x to 1.078/x.

Therefore, the integral from x=2 to x=6.5 of 1/sqrt(0.7x² + 0.3x) dx ≈ integral from 2 to 6.5 of [1.153/x - 1.078/x] dx ≈ integral [0.075/x] dx from 2 to 6.5

That integral is 0.075 * [ln(x)] from 2 to 6.5 ≈ 0.075*(ln(6.5) - ln(2)) ≈ 0.075*(1.8718 - 0.6931) ≈ 0.075*(1.1787) ≈ 0.0881.

But that's a very rough approximation. Maybe I can do better.

Alternatively, let's use the trapezoidal rule for the integral from x=2 to x=6.5.

Divide the interval [2, 6.5] into n subintervals. Let's pick n=2 for simplicity.

So, the points are x=2, x=4, x=6.5.

Compute the function at these points:

At x=2: f(2) = 1 / sqrt(0.7*4 + 0.3*2) = 1 / sqrt(2.8 + 0.6) = 1 / sqrt(3.4) ≈ 0.5345

At x=4: f(4) = 1 / sqrt(0.7*16 + 0.3*4) = 1 / sqrt(11.2 + 1.2) = 1 / sqrt(12.4) ≈ 0.4518

At x=6.5: f(6.5) = 1 / sqrt(0.7*42.25 + 0.3*6.5) = 1 / sqrt(29.575 + 1.95) = 1 / sqrt(31.525) ≈ 0.3565

Using the trapezoidal rule:

Integral ≈ (Δx / 2) [f(a) + 2f(midpoint) + f(b)]

Δx = 6.5 - 2 = 4.5

Midpoint is x=4.

So, ≈ (4.5 / 2) [0.5345 + 2*0.4518 + 0.3565] ≈ 2.25 [0.5345 + 0.9036 + 0.3565] ≈ 2.25 * 1.7946 ≈ 4.037

But that seems too low.

Wait, suppose the quasar is at z = 1.633. Let's compute the comoving distance.

The comoving distance D_c is given by:

D_c = (c / H0) * ∫(0 to z) [1 / sqrt(Ω_m(1 + z')^3 + Ω_λ(1 + z')^2)] dz'

But with Ω_m = 0.3, Ω_λ = 0.7.

First, let's define the integrand:

f(z') = 1 / sqrt(0.3*(1 + z')^3 + 0.7*(1 + z')^2)

Simplify the expression:

= 1 / sqrt(0.3(1 + z')^3 + 0.7(1 + z')^2)

Factor out (1 + z')^2:

= 1 / sqrt( (1 + z')^2 [0.3(1 + z') + 0.7] )

= 1 / [ (1 + z') * sqrt(0.3(1 + z') + 0.7) ]

So,

f(z') = 1 / [ (1 + z') * sqrt(0.3(1 + z') + 0.7) ]

So the integral becomes:

∫(0 to z) [1 / ( (1 + z') * sqrt(0.3(1 + z') + 0.7) ) ] dz'

Let me make a substitution. Let u = 1 + z', so when z' = 0, u = 1; when z' = z, u = 1 + z. Then, dz' = du.

So,

∫(1 to 1+z) [1 / ( u * sqrt(0.3u + 0.7) ) ] du

That's equivalent to:

∫(1 to 1+z) [1 / (u sqrt(0.3u + 0.7))] du

This integral can be solved by substitution.

Let me set t = sqrt(0.3u + 0.7). Then, t^2 = 0.3u + 0.7 => 2t dt = 0.3 du => du = (2t / 0.3) dt = (20/3) t dt.

So, the integral becomes:

∫(t1 to t2) [1 / (u * t)] * (20/3) t dt

But u = (t^2 - 0.7)/0.3, from t^2 = 0.3u + 0.7.

So, plugging u back in:

= ∫(t1 to t2) [1 / ( ( (t^2 - 0.7)/0.3 ) * t ) ] * (20/3) t dt

Simplify:

= ∫(t1 to t2) [ (0.3) / ( (t^2 - 0.7) ) ] * (20/3) dt

Simplify constants:

0.3 * (20/3) = 2

So,

= ∫(t1 to t2) [ 2 / (t^2 - 0.7) ] dt

Which is a standard integral:

= 2 * [ (1 / sqrt(0.7)) * sinh^{-1}(sqrt(0.7)/t) ) ] evaluated from t1 to t2.

Wait, actually, integral of 1/(t^2 - a^2) dt is (1/(2a)) ln |(t - a)/(t + a)| + C.

So, more accurately:

∫ [ 1 / (t^2 - a^2) ] dt = (1/(2a)) ln |(t - a)/(t + a)| + C

So, in our case, a^2 = 0.7, so a = sqrt(0.7) ≈ 0.8366.

So,

∫ [ 2 / (t^2 - 0.7) ] dt = 2 * [ (1/(2*sqrt(0.7))) ln |(t - sqrt(0.7))/(t + sqrt(0.7))| ) ] + C

= (1 / sqrt(0.7)) ln |(t - sqrt(0.7))/(t + sqrt(0.7))| ) + C

Thus, the integral is:

(1 / sqrt(0.7)) ln |(t - sqrt(0.7))/(t + sqrt(0.7))| ) evaluated from t = sqrt(0.7) to t = sqrt(0.3u + 0.7), which is t at u = 1 + z.

Wait, when u = 1, t = sqrt(0.3*1 + 0.7) = sqrt(1.0) = 1.

When u = 1 + z, t = sqrt(0.3*(1 + z) + 0.7).

So, putting it all together, the integral ∫(0 to z) f(z') dz' is:

[ (1 / sqrt(0.7)) ln( (t - sqrt(0.7))/(t + sqrt(0.7)) ) ] evaluated from t = 1 to t = sqrt(0.3*(1 + z) + 0.7)

So, compute at upper limit t2 = sqrt(0.3(1 + z) + 0.7):

= (1 / sqrt(0.7)) ln( (t2 - sqrt(0.7))/(t2 + sqrt(0.7)) )

At lower limit t1 = 1:

= (1 / sqrt(0.7)) ln( (1 - sqrt(0.7))/(1 + sqrt(0.7)) )

Thus, the integral is:

(1 / sqrt(0.7)) [ ln( (t2 - sqrt(0.7))/(t2 + sqrt(0.7)) ) - ln( (1 - sqrt(0.7))/(1 + sqrt(0.7)) ) ]

Simplify the expression:

= (1 / sqrt(0.7)) ln [ ( (t2 - sqrt(0.7))/(t2 + sqrt(0.7)) ) / ( (1 - sqrt(0.7))/(1 + sqrt(0.7)) ) ]

= (1 / sqrt(0.7)) ln [ ( (t2 - sqrt(0.7))(1 + sqrt(0.7)) ) / ( (t2 + sqrt(0.7))(1 - sqrt(0.7)) ) ]

So, putting it all together, D_c is:

D_c = (c / H0) * (1 / sqrt(0.7)) ln [ ( (t2 - sqrt(0.7))(1 + sqrt(0.7)) ) / ( (t2 + sqrt(0.7))(1 - sqrt(0.7)) ) ]

= sqrt(1.0 + 0.3 z)

So, t2 = sqrt(1 + 0.3 z)

Therefore, we can write the expression inside the log as:

[ (sqrt(1 + 0.3 z) - sqrt(0.7)) * (1 + sqrt(0.7)) ) ] / [ (sqrt(1 + 0.3 z) + sqrt(0.7)) * (1 - sqrt(0.7)) ) ]

Simplify numerator and denominator:

Let me denote A = sqrt(1 + 0.3 z), B = sqrt(0.7)

So, expression becomes:

[ (A - B)(1 + B) ] / [ (A + B)(1 - B) ]

Multiply numerator and denominator:

Numerator: (A - B)(1 + B) = A(1 + B) - B(1 + B) = A + A B - B - B^2

Denominator: (A + B)(1 - B) = A(1 - B) + B(1 - B) = A - A B + B - B^2

So, the expression is:

[ A + A B - B - B^2 ] / [ A - A B + B - B^2 ]

Factor terms:

Let me factor A and B:

Numerator:

A(1 + B) - B(1 + B) = (A - B)(1 + B)

Denominator: (A + B)(1 - B)

So, we can write:

[ (A - B)/(A + B) ) ] * [ (1 + B)/(1 - B) ]

Thus, expression becomes:

[ (A - B)/(A + B) ) ] * [ (1 + B)/(1 - B) ]

So, log of that is log( (A - B)/(A + B) ) + log( (1 + B)/(1 - B) )

First, I need to recall what comoving distance is. From what I remember, comoving distance is a measure of how far an object is from us, taking into account the expansion of the universe. It's like the distance that would have been the same if the universe had been expanding at a constant rate since the object was emitted. Unlike proper distance, which changes with time due to expansion, comoving distance is constant for a given object.

In a flat universe, which this one is, the comoving distance (DC) can be calculated using the integral of c/(H₀(E)) from the redshift (z) of the object to 0. Here, c is the speed of light, and H₀(E) is the Hubble parameter as a function of redshift.

First, I need to recall what comoving distance is. From what I remember, comoving distance is a concept in cosmology that represents the distance between two points in space as measured along the line of sight, taking into account the expansion of the universe. It's the distance that would have been the same if the universe were flat and the expansion of the universe didn't happen. This is also sometimes called the "proper distance" but I think comoving distance is a bit different because it's in co-moving coordinates.

The formula for comoving distance (DC) is an integral involving the Hubble parameter over the redshift. The general formula is:

DC = (1 / H0) * ∫(0 to z) [1 / sqrt(Ωm (1 + z') + ΩΛ)]^{-1} dz'

But since the universe is flat, Ωm + ΩΛ = 1, so the denominator becomes sqrt(Ωm (1 + z') + ΩΛ) = sqrt(Ωm + ΩΛ(1 + z')).

Wait, no, that's not right. Let me correct that. Since Ωm and ΩΛ are the matter and dark energy density parameters respectively, and for a flat universe, Ωm + ΩΛ = 1. So at any redshift z, the total matter and dark energy would be Ωm/(1 + z) + ΩΛ, right? Because the matter density scales as (1 + z)^-3, and dark energy is a cosmological constant, which doesn't scale. So the effective Hubble parameter at redshift z is H(z) = H0 * sqrt(Ωm (1 + z)^-3 + ΩΛ).

Therefore, the comoving distance is:

DC = (1 / H0) ∫(0 to z) [1 / sqrt(Ωm (1 + z')^{-3} + ΩΛ)] dz'

So, we need to integrate this from z=0 to z.

So, let's plug in the numbers.

Let me denote f(z') = 1 / sqrt(0.3 / (1 + z')^3 + 0.7)

We can compute this integral numerically.

First, let's compute it for z ≈ 0.58.

But actually, let's see what the integral would look like.

Alternatively, perhaps we can use a substitution. Let me set a = 1 + z, so when z=0, a=1, and when z=z, a=1+z.

Then, dz = da / a

So, the integral becomes:

∫(1 to 1 + z) [1 / sqrt(0.3 / a^3 + 0.7)] * (da / a)

Simplify the integrand:

[1 / sqrt(0.3 / a^3 + 0.7)] * (1 / a) = 1 / [a sqrt(0.3 / a^3 + 0.7)]

Simplify inside the sqrt:

0.3 / a^3 + 0.7 = (0.3 + 0.7 a^3) / a^3

So sqrt(0.3 / a^3 + 0.7) = sqrt((0.3 + 0.7 a^3)/a^3) = sqrt(0.3 + 0.7 a^3) / a^(3/2)

Therefore, the integrand becomes:

First, let me recall what comoving distance means. Comoving distance is the distance that a light beam would travel in a universe without expansion (i.e., in a comoving frame) to reach an object at a certain redshift. It's a way to measure distances that accounts for the expansion of the universe.

The formula for comoving distance (DC) in a flat universe is:

DC = (c / H0) * ∫(0 to z) [1 / √(Ω_m(1 + z')^3 + Ω_Λ)] dz'

Where c is the speed of light, H0 is the Hubble constant, and z is the redshift.

At z=0.5: f(0.5)=sqrt(0.3*(1.5)^3 + 0.7)=sqrt(0.3*3.375 +0.7)=sqrt(1.0125 +0.7)=sqrt(1.7125)≈1.309

We can approximate this integral using the trapezoidal rule or Simpson's rule.

Alternatively, for small z, the integral can be approximated as:

∫0^z dz' / sqrt(a + b z') ≈ z / sqrt(a + b z)

But in this case, b is 0.3*(3)(1)^2=0.9, but it's inside the square root, so it's more complicated.

Alternatively, for small z, the integrand can be approximated as:

1 / sqrt(0.3*(1 + 3z) + 0.7) ≈ 1 / sqrt(0.3 + 0.9z + 0.7) = 1 / sqrt(1 + 0.9z)

Using a Taylor expansion for 1 / sqrt(1 + x) ≈ 1 - 0.5x + 0.125x^2 - ..., so:

≈1 - 0.5*(0.9z) + ...=1 - 0.45z

So integrating from 0 to z:

∫0^z (1 - 0.45z') dz' = z - 0.45*(z^2)/2 = z - 0.225 z^2

Those have to do with the absorption of light at certain wavelengths because of the expansion of the universe. The Gunn-Peterson effect occurs when the intergalactic medium has a high enough neutral hydrogen density to cause absorption at a certain redshift. The Lyman limit is around 91 nm, which is in the far ultraviolet. If the quasar's spectrum is dropping at 790 nm, which is in the near infrared, that's much longer than the Lyman limit. Okay, so I have this problem about a quasar observed in the near-infrared and optical spectrum. The peak flux is at around 790 nm, and the flux drops significantly at shorter wavelengths. The question is about determining the comoving distance of this quasar from Earth in a universe described by the Lambda-CDM model. The given parameters are Hubble constant H0 = 70 km/s/Mpc, matter density Ω_m = 0.3, dark energy density Ω_Λ = 0.7, and a flat universe. The correct answer is supposed to be 9 Gpc, but I need to figure out why.

First, let me recall some concepts. A quasar is a highly luminous and compact region emitting with a very high energy output. The fact that the flux peaks at 790 nm suggests that we might be looking at the rest frame of the quasar. In the rest frame, the peak of the emission (the so-called "minimum" in the spectrum, where the flux drops at shorter wavelengths) is around 790 nm. If the quasar is moving away from us (due to the expansion of the universe), this peak wavelength would be redshifted.

So, the first step is probably to calculate the redshift of the quasar. Once we have the redshift, we can compute the comoving distance using the Hubble parameter and the given cosmological parameters.

The redshift can be determined using the relationship between the observed wavelength (λ_obs) and the rest wavelength (λ_rest). The formula is:

λ_obs = λ_rest * (1 + z)

But wait, actually, the relationship is λ_obs = (1 + z) * λ_rest only if the redshift is small and if we are considering the shift in wavelength due to the expansion of the universe. For larger redshifts, more precise expressions might be needed.

But in this case, 790 nm is in the near-infrared part of the spectrum. Let me see what the rest frame would imply for the quasar.

If the peak in the rest frame is around 790 nm, then in our observed frame, it would be stretched by a factor of (1 + z). So if we can measure the observed peak, we can get z.

But wait, the problem says that the peak is at 790 nm, and at shorter wavelengths, the flux drops. So actually, in the rest frame, the peak is at around 790 nm, but in the observed frame, it's longer. Wait, that seems contradictory. If the quasar is moving away, the wavelength should be longer, so the peak in the observed frame would be at a longer wavelength than 790 nm. Alright, so I have this question about a quasar observed in the near-infrared and optical spectrum. The peak flux is at around 790 nm, and it drops significantly at shorter wavelengths. The question is about the comoving distance of this quasar from Earth in a Lambda-CDM universe. The given parameters are Hubble constant H0 = 70 km s^-1 Mpc^-1, matter density Ω_m = 0.3, dark energy density Ω_Λ = 0.7, and the universe is flat. The correct answer is supposed to be 9 Gpc, but I need to figure out why.

First, I should recall what comoving distance means. From what I remember, comoving distance is a way to measure distances in a expanding universe, taking into account the expansion factor over time. It's defined as the integral of the inverse of the Hubble parameter over redshift. So, the formula is:

But how do I find the comoving distance?

In a flat Lambda-CDM universe, the comoving distance as a function of redshift is given by:

DC = (1 / H0) * integral from 0 to z of 1 / sqrt(E(z')) dz'

Where E(z) = sqrt(Ωm*(1 + z)^3 + ΩΛ)

Given that Ωm = 0.3, ΩΛ = 0.7, and H0 = 70 km/s/Mpc.

So, we can compute this integral numerically for z ≈ 0.129.

Alternatively, since the redshift is small (z ≈ 0.129), maybe we can approximate the integral.

First, compute E(z) at z = 0.129:

E(z) = sqrt(0.3*(1 + 0.129)^3 + 0.7)

Compute (1 + z) = 1.129

(1.129)^3 ≈ 1.129 * 1.129 ≈ 1.276 * 1.129 ≈ 1.434

So, 0.3 * 1.434 ≈ 0.430

Then, E(z) = sqrt(0.430 + 0.7) = sqrt(1.130) ≈ 1.063

So, E(z) ≈ 1.063

Therefore, 1/E(z) ≈ 1 / 1.063 ≈ 0.939

Then, the integral from 0 to z of 1/E(z') dz'

Since z is small, we can approximate the integral as 1/E(0) * z, but actually, E(z) changes with z, so it's not exactly correct.

But another approximation for small z is to expand E(z) as a Taylor series around z = 0.

E(z) ≈ E(0) + E'(0) * z + ...

But E(0) = sqrt(0.3 + 0.7) = sqrt(1) = 1.

E'(z) = (1/2) * [ (3 Ωm (1 + z)^2) / (Ωm (1 + z)^3 + ΩΛ) ) + 0 ] ?

Wait, maybe better to compute the derivative of E(z):

E(z) = sqrt(0.3*(1 + z)^3 + 0.7)

So, dE/dz = (1/(2*sqrt(0.3*(1 + z)^3 + 0.7))) * 0.3*3*(1 + z)^2

= (0.45*(1 + z)^2) / sqrt(0.3*(1 + z)^3 + 0.7)

At z = 0, dE/dz = 0.45 / sqrt(0.3 + 0.7) = 0.45 / 1 = 0.45.

So, E(z) ≈ 1 + 0.45*z + ... for small z.

Therefore, 1/E(z) ≈ 1 - 0.45*z + ...

Therefore, the integral from 0 to z of 1/E(z') dz' ≈ integral from 0 to z [1 - 0.45*z'] dz'

= z - 0.45*(z^2)/2

= z - 0.225 z^2

With z ≈ 0.129, z^2 ≈ 0.0167

So, the integral ≈ 0.129 - 0.225 * 0.0167 ≈ 0.129 - 0.00378 ≈ 0.125

Therefore, the comoving distance DC ≈ (1 / H0) * 0.125

H0 is 70 km/s/Mpc. To convert H0 to s^-1, we use H0 = 70 km/s/Mpc = 70e3 m/s / (3.086e22 m / Mpc) ) ≈ 70e3 / 3.086e22 ≈ 2.27e-19 s^-1

Alright, so I have this question about a quasar observed in the near-infrared and optical spectrum. The peak wavelength is around 790 nm, and the flux drops significantly at shorter wavelengths than that. The question is asking about the comoving distance of this quasar from Earth in a Lambda-CDM universe with specific parameters: Hubble constant (H0) is 70 km/s/Mpc, matter density parameter (Omega_m) is 0.3, dark energy density parameter (Omega_lambda) is 0.7, and the universe is flat.

Hmm, okay. So first, I need to recall what a comoving distance is. From what I remember, comoving distance is a concept in cosmology that represents the distance between two points in space as measured along the line of sight, taking into account the expansion of the universe. So, it's the distance we would measure today if we could somehow "freeze" the expansion of the universe at the time the light we receive today was emitted.

Given that the universe is flat, the comoving distance can be calculated using the integral of the Hubble parameter over the redshift. The formula is something like:

D_c = (1/c) * ∫_0^z (1/(H0 * E(z))) dz

Where E(z) is the effective potential, which is a function of the matter and dark energy parameters. For a flat universe dominated by matter and dark energy, E(z) is given by:

E(z) = sqrt(Omega_m*(1+z)^3 + Omega_lambda*(1+z)^(-3*w)

Wait, but dark energy typically has an equation of state w that's about -1 for a cosmological constant. So, plugging in, that simplifies to:

E(z) = sqrt(Omega_m*(1+z)^3 + Omega_lambda)

Because (1+z)^(-3*w) becomes (1+z)^3 when w=-1.

So, in this case, Omega_m is 0.3 and Omega_lambda is 0.7, which adds up to 1 because the universe is flat. So, E(z) would be:

E(z) = sqrt(0.3*(1+z)^3 + 0.7)

The comoving distance (DC) is given by:

where c is the speed of light, z is the redshift, and H(z) is the Hubble parameter at redshift z.

At z=4:

At z=5:

At z=6:

At z=7:

At z=8:

Wait, let's see, from z=0 to z=25, f(z) decreases from 1 to approximately 0.015. The function is smoothly decreasing, but the rate of decrease is slowing down.

To approximate the integral ∫(0 to z) f(z') dz' ≈ ?

We can use the trapezoidal rule with a few intervals. Let's choose n=10 intervals for simplicity.

Let me choose z=0,5,10,15,20,25.

Compute f(z) at these points:

z=0: f=1

z=5: f≈0.181

z=10: f≈0.046

z=15: f≈0.026

z=20: f≈0.019

z=25: f≈0.015

Wait, but between z=20 and z=25, f(z) decreases from 0.019 to 0.015, which is still a decrease, but very slow.

Using the trapezoidal rule:

Integral ≈ (Δz/2) [f(z0) + 2f(z1) + 2f(z2) + ... + 2f(zn-1) + f(zn)]

Δz = 5.

So,

Integral ≈ (5/2) [1 + 2*(0.181 + 0.046 + 0.026 + 0.019 + 0.015) + 0.015]

Compute the sum inside:

2*(0.181 + 0.046 + 0.026 + 0.019 + 0.015) = 2*(0.307) = 0.614

So,

Integral ≈ (5/2)*(1 + 0.614 + 0.015) = (2.5)*(1.629) ≈ 4.0725

But this is an underestimate because the function is decreasing, and trapezoidal rule tends to underestimate when the function is concave down. So, perhaps a better approximation is Simpson's rule, which uses parabolic segments.

Simpson's rule for n=10 (even number of intervals):

Integral ≈ (Δz/3) [f(z0) + 4f(z1) + 2f(z2) + 4f(z3) + 2f(z4) + 4f(z5) + 2f(z6) + 4f(z7) + 2f(z8) + 4f(z9) + f(z10)]

So, with n=10, which is even, Simpson's rule can be applied.

Compute the coefficients:

z0=0: 1

z1=5: 4*(0.181) = 0.724

z2=10: 2*(0.046) = 0.092

z3=15:4*(0.026)=0.104

z4=20:2*(0.019)=0.038

z5=25:4*(0.015)=0.06

z6=30:2*f(30)

Wait, but we only have data up to z=25. To apply Simpson's rule accurately, we should have an even number of intervals, which we have (n=10), but we need to go up to z=25, which is the 5th interval (since n=10, Δz=5).
